<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Musical instrument</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Device created or adapted to make musical sounds</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attributes_of_Music.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Attributes_of_Music.jpg/300px-Attributes_of_Music.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Attributes_of_Music.jpg/450px-Attributes_of_Music.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Attributes_of_Music.jpg/600px-Attributes_of_Music.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attributes_of_Music.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anne_Vallayer-Coster" title="Anne Vallayer-Coster">Anne Vallayer-Coster</a>, <i>Attributes of Music</i>, 1770. This <a href="/wiki/Still_life" title="Still life">still life</a> painting depicts a variety of French Baroque musical instruments, such as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_horn" title="Natural horn">natural horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transverse_flute" title="Transverse flute">transverse flute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musette_de_cour" title="Musette de cour">musette</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pardessus_de_viole" title="Pardessus de viole">pardessus de viole</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>musical instrument</b> is a device created or adapted to make <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">musical sounds</a>. In principle, any object that produces sound can be considered a musical instrument—it is through purpose that the object becomes a musical instrument. A person who plays a musical instrument is known as an <b>instrumentalist</b>. The history of musical instruments dates to the beginnings of human culture. Early musical instruments may have been used for rituals, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horn_(music)" title="Horn (music)">horn</a> to signal success on the hunt, or a <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drum</a> in a religious ceremony. Cultures eventually developed composition and performance of <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodies</a> for entertainment. Musical instruments evolved in step with changing applications and technologies.
</p><p>The date and origin of the first device considered a musical instrument is disputed. The oldest object that some scholars refer to as a musical instrument, <a href="/wiki/Divje_Babe_flute" title="Divje Babe flute">a simple flute</a>, dates back as far as 50,000 - 60,000 years. Some consensus dates <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_flutes" title="Paleolithic flutes">early flutes</a> to about 40,000 years ago. However, most historians believe that determining a specific time of musical instrument invention is impossible, as many early musical instruments were made from animal skins, bone, wood, and other non-durable materials.
</p><p>Musical instruments developed independently in many populated regions of the world. However, contact among civilizations caused rapid spread and adaptation of most instruments in places far from their origin. By the <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">post-classical era</a>, instruments from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> were in <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">maritime Southeast Asia</a>, and Europeans played instruments originating from <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>. Development in the Americas occurred at a slower pace, but cultures of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> shared musical instruments. 
</p><p>By 1400, musical instrument development slowed in many areas and was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Occident</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a> periods of music, lasting from roughly 1750 to 1900, many new musical instruments were developed. While the evolution of traditional musical instruments slowed beginning in the 20th century, the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> led to the invention of new electric instruments, such as <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Theremin" title="Theremin">theremin</a>.
</p><p>Musical instrument classification is a discipline in its own right, and many systems of classification have been used over the years. Instruments can be classified by their effective range, material composition, size, role, etc. However, the most common academic method, <a href="/wiki/Hornbostel%E2%80%93Sachs" title="Hornbostel–Sachs">Hornbostel–Sachs</a>, uses the means by which they produce sound. The academic study of musical instruments is called <a href="/wiki/Organology" title="Organology">organology</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_basic_operation">Definition and basic operation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition and basic operation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TamTam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/TamTam.jpg/225px-TamTam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/TamTam.jpg/338px-TamTam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/TamTam.jpg/450px-TamTam.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TamTam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamileke" title="Bamileke">Bamileke</a> <a href="/wiki/Slit_drum" title="Slit drum">slit drum</a> drummers in Cameroon's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Province_(Cameroon)" title="West Province (Cameroon)">West Province</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A musical instrument is used to make <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">musical sounds</a>. Once humans moved from making sounds with their bodies — for example, by clapping—to using objects to create music from sounds, musical instruments were born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu1-1">[1]</a></sup> Primitive instruments were probably designed to emulate natural sounds, and their purpose was ritual rather than entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault9_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault9-2">[2]</a></sup> The concept of melody and the artistic pursuit of musical composition were probably unknown to early players of musical instruments. A person sounding a <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_flutes" title="Paleolithic flutes">bone flute</a> to signal the start of a hunt does so without thought of the modern notion of "making music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault9_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rault9-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Musical instruments are constructed in a broad array of styles and shapes, using many different materials. Early musical instruments were made from "found objects" such as shells and plant parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault9_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rault9-2">[2]</a></sup> As instruments evolved, so did the selection and quality of materials. Virtually every material in nature has been used by at least one culture to make musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault9_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rault9-2">[2]</a></sup> One plays a musical instrument by interacting with it in some way — for example, by plucking the strings on a <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">string instrument</a>, striking the surface of a drum, or blowing into an animal horn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault9_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rault9-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">Archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Researchers have discovered archaeological evidence of musical instruments in many parts of the world. Some artifacts have been dated to 67,000 years old, while critics often dispute the findings. Consensus solidifying about artifacts dated back to around 37,000 years old and later. Artifacts made from durable materials, or constructed using durable methods, have been found to survive. As such, the specimens found cannot be irrefutably placed as the earliest musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blades34_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blades34-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_(mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie,_Ljubljana)_(9420310527).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg/220px-Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg/330px-Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg/440px-Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_%28mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie%2C_Ljubljana%29_%289420310527%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fl%C3%BBte_pal%C3%A9olithique_(mus%C3%A9e_national_de_Slov%C3%A9nie,_Ljubljana)_(9420310527).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Found in <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divje_Babe_Flute" title="Divje Babe Flute">Divje Babe Flute</a> is sometimes considered the world's <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_flutes" title="Paleolithic flutes">oldest known musical instrument</a></div></div></div>
<p>In July 1995, Slovenian archaeologist Ivan Turk discovered a <a href="/wiki/Bone_carving" title="Bone carving">bone carving</a> in the northwest region of <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>. The carving, named the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divje_Babe_Flute" title="Divje Babe Flute">Divje Babe Flute</a>, features four holes that Canadian <a href="/wiki/Musicology" title="Musicology">musicologist</a> Bob Fink determined could have been used to play four notes of a <a href="/wiki/Diatonic_scale" title="Diatonic scale">diatonic scale</a>. Researchers estimate the flute's age at between 43,400 and 67,000 years old, making it the oldest known musical instrument and the only musical instrument associated with <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAS_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAS-4">[4]</a></sup> However, some archaeologists and <a href="/wiki/Ethnomusicology" title="Ethnomusicology">ethnomusicologists</a> dispute the flute's status as a musical instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chase_and_Nowell_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chase_and_Nowell-5">[5]</a></sup> German archaeologists have found <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> bone and <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swan</a> bone flutes dating back to 30,000 to 37,000 years old in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swabian_Alps" title="Swabian Alps">Swabian Alps</a>. The flutes were made in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> age, and are more commonly accepted as being the oldest known musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBC_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBC-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological evidence of musical instruments was discovered in excavations at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Cemetery_at_Ur" title="Royal Cemetery at Ur">Royal Cemetery</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>. These instruments, one of the first ensembles of instruments yet discovered, include nine <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyres</a> ( the <a href="/wiki/Lyres_of_Ur" title="Lyres of Ur">Lyres of Ur</a>), two <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harps</a>, a silver double <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sistrum" title="Sistrum">sistrum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cymbal" title="Cymbal">cymbals</a>. A set of reed-sounded silver pipes discovered in Ur was the likely predecessor of modern <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collinson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-7">[7]</a></sup> The cylindrical pipes feature three side-holes that allowed players to produce a <a href="/wiki/Whole-tone_scale" title="Whole-tone scale">whole-tone scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell82_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell82-8">[8]</a></sup> These excavations, carried out by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woolley" title="Leonard Woolley">Leonard Woolley</a> in the 1920s, uncovered non-degradable fragments of instruments and the voids left by the degraded segments that, together, have been used to reconstruct them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Schauensee_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Schauensee-9">[9]</a></sup> The graves these instruments were buried in have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dated" title="Carbon dated">carbon dated</a> to between 2600 and 2500 BC, providing evidence that these instruments were used in Sumeria by this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moorey_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moorey-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeologists in the <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a> site of central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henan_province" title="Henan province">Henan province</a> of China have found flutes made of bones that date back 7,000 to 9,000 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bnl_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-bnl-11">[11]</a></sup> representing some of the "earliest complete, playable, tightly-dated, multinote musical instruments" ever found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bnl_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-bnl-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_music" title="History of music">History of music</a></div>
<p>Scholars agree that there are no completely reliable methods of determining the exact chronology of musical instruments across cultures. Comparing and organizing instruments based on their complexity is misleading, since advancements in musical instruments have sometimes reduced complexity. For example, construction of early slit drums involved felling and hollowing out large trees; later <a href="/wiki/Slit_drum" title="Slit drum">slit drums</a> were made by opening bamboo stalks, a much simpler task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs60_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs60-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>German musicologist <a href="/wiki/Curt_Sachs" title="Curt Sachs">Curt Sachs</a>, one of the most prominent <a href="/wiki/Musicology" title="Musicology">musicologists</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnomusicology" title="Ethnomusicology">musical ethnologists</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baines37_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baines37-15">[15]</a></sup> in modern times, argues that it is misleading to arrange the development of musical instruments by workmanship, since cultures advance at different rates and have access to different raw materials. For example, contemporary <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a> comparing musical instruments from two cultures that existed at the same time but differed in organization, culture, and handicraft <a href="/wiki/Anthropological_theories_of_value" title="Anthropological theories of value">cannot determine which instruments are more "primitive"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs61_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs61-16">[16]</a></sup> Ordering instruments by geography is also not reliable, as it cannot always be determined when and how cultures contacted one another and shared knowledge. Sachs proposed that a geographical chronology until approximately 1400 is preferable, however, due to its limited subjectivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs63_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs63-17">[17]</a></sup> Beyond 1400, one can follow the overall development of musical instruments over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs63_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs63-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The science of marking the order of musical instrument development relies on archaeological artifacts, artistic depictions, and literary references. Since data in one research path can be inconclusive, all three paths provide a better historical picture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blades34_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blades34-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric">Prehistoric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Prehistoric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_music" title="Prehistoric music">Prehistoric music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_Teponaztli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1576" data-file-width="1716" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Two_Teponaztli.jpg/220px-Two_Teponaztli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Two_Teponaztli.jpg/330px-Two_Teponaztli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Two_Teponaztli.jpg/440px-Two_Teponaztli.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_Teponaztli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> slit drums (<i><a href="/wiki/Teponaztli" title="Teponaztli">teponaztli</a></i>). The characteristic "<b>H</b>" slits can be seen on the top of the drum in the foreground.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guitar_(molo).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1936" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg/220px-Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg/330px-Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg/440px-Guitar_%28molo%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guitar_(molo).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Guitar (molo)</div></div></div>
<p>Until the 19th century AD, European-written music histories began with mythological accounts mingled with scripture of how musical instruments were invented. Such accounts included <a href="/wiki/Jubal_(Bible)" title="Jubal (Bible)">Jubal</a>, descendant of <a href="/wiki/Cain" title="Cain">Cain</a> and "father of all such as handle the harp and the organ" (<a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">Genesis</a> 4:21) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_(mythology)" title="Pan (mythology)">Pan</a>, inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Pan_flute" title="Pan flute">pan pipes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a>, who is said to have made a dried <a href="/wiki/Tortoise" title="Tortoise">tortoise</a> shell into the first <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>. Modern histories have replaced such mythology with anthropological speculation, occasionally informed by archeological evidence. Scholars agree that there was no definitive "invention" of the musical instrument since the term "musical instrument" is subjective and hard to define.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs297_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs297-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the first devices external to the human body that are considered instruments are <a href="/wiki/Rattle_(percussion_instrument)" title="Rattle (percussion instrument)">rattles</a>, stampers, and various <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drums</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blades36_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blades36-19">[19]</a></sup> These instruments evolved due to the human motor impulse to add sound to emotional movements such as dancing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs26_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs26-20">[20]</a></sup> Eventually, some cultures assigned ritual functions to their musical instruments, using them for hunting and various ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault34_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault34-21">[21]</a></sup> Those cultures developed more complex <a href="/wiki/Percussion_instrument" title="Percussion instrument">percussion instruments</a> and other instruments such as ribbon reeds, flutes, and trumpets. Some of these labels carry far different connotations from those used in modern day; early flutes and trumpets are so-labeled for their basic operation and function rather than resemblance to modern instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs34_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs34-22">[22]</a></sup> Among early cultures for whom drums developed ritual, even sacred importance are the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>, the indigenous people of <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a>, and many cultures of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. In fact, drums were pervasive throughout every African culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blades51_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blades51-23">[23]</a></sup> One East African tribe, the <a href="/wiki/Wahinda" title="Wahinda">Wahinda</a>, believed it was so holy that seeing a drum would be fatal to any person other than the sultan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs35_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs35-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans eventually developed the concept of using musical instruments to produce <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melody</a>, which was previously common only in singing. Similar to the process of <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">reduplication</a> in language, instrument players first developed repetition and then arrangement. An early form of melody was produced by pounding two stamping tubes of slightly different sizes—one tube would produce a "clear" sound and the other would answer with a "darker" sound. Such instrument pairs also included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bullroarer_(music)" title="Bullroarer (music)">bullroarers</a>, slit drums, <a href="/wiki/Conch_(instrument)" title="Conch (instrument)">shell trumpets</a>, and skin drums. Cultures who used these instrument pairs associated them with gender; the "father" was the bigger or more energetic instrument, while the "mother" was the smaller or duller instrument. Musical instruments existed in this form for thousands of years before patterns of three or more tones would evolve in the form of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Xylophone" title="Xylophone">xylophone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs52_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs52-25">[25]</a></sup> Xylophones originated in the mainland and archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, eventually spreading to Africa, the Americas, and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse24_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse24-26">[26]</a></sup> Along with xylophones, which ranged from simple sets of three "leg bars" to carefully tuned sets of parallel bars, various cultures developed instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">ground harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">ground zither</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musical_bow" title="Musical bow">musical bow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jew%27s_harp" title="Jew's harp">jaw harp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs53_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs53-27">[27]</a></sup> Recent research into usage wear and acoustics of stone artefacts has revealed a possible new class of prehistoric musical instrument, known as <a href="/wiki/Lithophone" title="Lithophone">lithophones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_music" title="Ancient music">Ancient music</a></div>
<p>Images of musical instruments begin to appear in Mesopotamian artifacts in 2800 BC or earlier. Beginning around 2000 BC, <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylonian</a> cultures began delineating two distinct classes of musical instruments due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_of_labor" title="Division of labor">division of labor</a> and the evolving class system. Popular instruments, simple and playable by anyone, evolved differently from professional instruments whose development focused on effectiveness and skill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs67_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs67-30">[30]</a></sup> Despite this development, very few musical instruments have been recovered in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. Scholars must rely on artifacts and <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> texts written in <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> to reconstruct the early history of musical instruments in Mesopotamia. Even the process of assigning names to these instruments is challenging since there is no clear distinction among various instruments and the words used to describe them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs68_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs68-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Sumerian and Babylonian artists mainly depicted ceremonial instruments, historians have distinguished six <a href="/wiki/Idiophone" title="Idiophone">idiophones</a> used in early Mesopotamia: concussion clubs, clappers, <a href="/wiki/Sistrum" title="Sistrum">sistra</a>, bells, cymbals, and rattles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs69_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs69-32">[32]</a></sup> Sistra are depicted prominently in a great relief of <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_III" title="Amenhotep III">Amenhotep III</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remnant168_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remnant168-33">[33]</a></sup> and are of particular interest because similar designs have been found in far-reaching places such as <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and among the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> <a href="/wiki/Yaqui" title="Yaqui">Yaqui</a> tribe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs70_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs70-34">[34]</a></sup> The people of Mesopotamia preferred <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">stringed instruments</a>, as evidenced by their proliferation in Mesopotamian figurines, plaques, and seals. Innumerable varieties of harps are depicted, as well as lyres and lutes, the forerunner of modern stringed instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs82_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs82-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="641" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg/220px-Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg/330px-Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg/440px-Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_lute_players_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptian</a> tomb painting depicting lute players, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eighteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt">18th Dynasty</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1350</span> BC)</div></div></div>
<p>Musical instruments used by the Egyptian culture before 2700 BC bore striking similarity to those of Mesopotamia, leading historians to conclude that the civilizations must have been in contact with one another. Sachs notes that Egypt did not possess any instruments that the Sumerian culture did not also possess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs86_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs86-36">[36]</a></sup> However, by 2700 BC the cultural contacts seem to have dissipated; the lyre, a prominent ceremonial instrument in Sumer, did not appear in Egypt for another 800 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs86_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs86-36">[36]</a></sup> Clappers and concussion sticks appear on Egyptian vases as early as 3000 BC. The civilization also made use of sistra, <a href="/wiki/Vertical_flute" title="Vertical flute">vertical flutes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Double_clarinet" title="Double clarinet">double clarinets</a>, arched and angular harps, and various drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault71_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault71-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Little history is available in the period between 2700 BC and 1500 BC, as Egypt (and indeed, Babylon) entered a long violent period of war and destruction. This period saw the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> destroy the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian empire</a> in Mesopotamia and the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> destroy the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom of Egypt</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaohs of Egypt</a> conquered Southwest Asia in around 1500 BC, the cultural ties to Mesopotamia were renewed and Egypt's musical instruments also reflected heavy influence from Asiatic cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs86_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs86-36">[36]</a></sup> Under their new cultural influences, the people of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">New Kingdom</a> began using <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, trumpets, <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lutes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castanets" title="Castanets">castanets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cymbal" title="Cymbal">cymbals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs98_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs98-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike Mesopotamia and Egypt, professional musicians did not exist in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> between 2000 and 1000 BC. While the history of musical instruments in Mesopotamia and Egypt relies on artistic representations, the culture in Israel produced few such representations. Scholars must therefore rely on information gleaned from the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs105_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs105-39">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_literature" title="Hebrew literature">Hebrew texts</a> mention two prominent instruments associated with <a href="/wiki/Jubal_(Bible)" title="Jubal (Bible)">Jubal</a>: the <i>ugab</i> (pipes) and <i><a href="/wiki/Kinnor" title="Kinnor">kinnor</a></i> (lyre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs106_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs106-40">[40]</a></sup> Other instruments of the period included the <i>tof</i> (<a href="/wiki/Frame_drum" title="Frame drum">frame drum</a>), <i>pa'amon</i> (small bells or jingles), <a href="/wiki/Shofar" title="Shofar">shofar</a>, and the trumpet-like <i>hasosra</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs108_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs108-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of a monarchy in Israel during the 11th century BC produced the first professional musicians and with them a drastic increase in the number and variety of musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs114_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs114-42">[42]</a></sup> However, identifying and classifying the instruments remains a challenge due to the lack of artistic interpretations. For example, stringed instruments of uncertain design called nevals and asors existed, but neither archaeology nor etymology can clearly define them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs116_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs116-43">[43]</a></sup> In her book <i>A Survey of Musical Instruments</i>, American musicologist Sibyl Marcuse proposes that the nevel must be similar to vertical harp due to its relation to <i>nabla</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> term for "harp".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse385_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse385-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a>, the use and development of musical instruments stood in stark contrast to those cultures' achievements in architecture and sculpture. The instruments of the time were simple and virtually all of them were imported from other cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs128_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs128-45">[45]</a></sup> Lyres were the principal instrument, as musicians used them to honor the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs129_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs129-46">[46]</a></sup> Greeks played a variety of <a href="/wiki/Wind_instrument" title="Wind instrument">wind instruments</a> they classified as <i><a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a></i> (reeds) or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrinx_(instrument)" title="Syrinx (instrument)">syrinx</a></i> (flutes); Greek writing from that time reflects a serious study of reed production and playing technique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell82_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell82-8">[8]</a></sup> Romans played reed instruments named <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibia_(instrument)" title="Tibia (instrument)">tibia</a></i>, featuring side-holes that could be opened or closed, allowing for greater flexibility in playing modes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell83_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell83-47">[47]</a></sup> Other instruments in common use in the region included vertical harps derived from those of the <a href="/wiki/Orient" title="Orient">Orient</a>, lutes of Egyptian design, various pipes and organs, and clappers, which were played primarily by women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs149_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs149-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence of musical instruments in use by early civilizations of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is almost completely lacking, making it impossible to reliably attribute instruments to the <a href="/wiki/Munda_languages" title="Munda languages">Munda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian</a> language-speaking cultures that first settled the area. Rather, the history of musical instruments in the area begins with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a> that emerged around 3000 BC. Various rattles and whistles found among excavated artifacts are the only physical evidence of musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs151_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs151-49">[49]</a></sup> A clay statuette indicates the use of drums, and examination of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script</a> has also revealed representations of vertical arched harps identical in design to those depicted in Sumerian artifacts. This discovery is among many indications that the Indus Valley and Sumerian cultures maintained cultural contact. Subsequent developments in musical instruments in India occurred with the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a>, or hymns. These songs used various drums, shell trumpets, harps, and flutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs152_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs152-50">[50]</a></sup> Other prominent instruments in use during the early centuries AD were the <a href="/wiki/Snake_charming" title="Snake charming">snake charmer's</a> <a href="/wiki/Double_clarinet" title="Double clarinet">double clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>, barrel drums, cross flutes, and short lutes. In all, India had no unique musical instruments until the <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">post-classical era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs161_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs161-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/220px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/330px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg/440px-%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B9%96%E5%8C%97%E5%8D%9A%E7%89%A9%E9%A4%A8%E6%9B%BE%E4%BE%AF%E4%B9%99%E7%B7%A8%E9%90%98.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The monumental <a href="/wiki/Bianzhong_of_Marquis_Yi_of_Zeng" title="Bianzhong of Marquis Yi of Zeng">Bianzhong of Marquis Yi of Zeng</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 5th century</span> BCE, from <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a></div></div></div>
<p>Musical instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zithers</a> appeared in Chinese writings around 12th century BC and earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs185_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs185-52">[52]</a></sup> Early <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (551–479 BC), <a href="/wiki/Mencius" title="Mencius">Mencius</a> (372–289 BC), and <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> shaped the development of musical instruments in China, adopting an attitude toward music similar to that of the Greeks. The Chinese believed that music was an essential part of character and community, and developed a unique system of classifying their musical instruments according to their material makeup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs162_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs162-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Idiophone" title="Idiophone">Idiophones</a> were extremely important in Chinese music, hence the majority of early instruments were idiophones. Poetry of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> mentions bells, chimes, drums, and globular flutes carved from bone, the latter of which has been excavated and preserved by archaeologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs166_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs166-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> saw percussion instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Clapper_(musical_instrument)" title="Clapper (musical instrument)">clappers</a>, troughs, <a href="/wiki/Wooden_fish" title="Wooden fish">wooden fish</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Yu_(percussion_instrument)" title="Yu (percussion instrument)">yǔ</a></i> (wooden tiger). Wind instruments such as flute, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan-pipe" title="Pan-pipe">pan-pipes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch-pipe" title="Pitch-pipe">pitch-pipes</a>, and mouth organs also appeared in this time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs178_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs178-55">[55]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Xiao_(flute)" title="Xiao (flute)">xiao</a></i> (an <a href="/wiki/End-blown_flute" title="End-blown flute">end-blown flute</a>) and various other instruments that spread through many cultures, came into use in China during and after the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs189_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs189-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg/220px-CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg/330px-CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg/440px-CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CarnyxDeTintignac2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carnyx discovered in <a href="/wiki/Tintignac" title="Tintignac">Tintignac</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although civilizations in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> attained a relatively high level of sophistication by the eleventh century AD, they lagged behind other civilizations in the development of musical instruments. For example, they had no stringed instruments; all of their instruments were idiophones, drums, and wind instruments such as flutes and trumpets. Of these, only the flute was capable of producing a melody.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs192_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs192-57">[57]</a></sup> In contrast, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South American</a> civilizations in areas such as modern-day <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> were less advanced culturally but more advanced musically. South American cultures of the time used pan-pipes as well as varieties of flutes, idiophones, drums, and shell or wood trumpets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs196_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs196-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>An instrument that can be attested to the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Carnyx" title="Carnyx">carnyx</a> which is dated to ~300 BC, the elongated trumpet-like instrument which had the end of the bell crafted from bronze into the shape of a screaming animal head which was held high above their heads, when blown into, the carnyx would emit a deep, harsh sound, the head also had a tongue which clicked when vibrated, the intention of the instrument was to use it on the battleground to intimidate their opponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Post-classical_era.2FMiddle_Ages"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_era/Middle_Ages">Post-classical era/Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Post-classical era/Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the period of time loosely referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">post-classical era</a> and Europe in particular as the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, China developed a tradition of integrating musical influence from other regions. The first record of this type of influence is in 384 AD, when China established an orchestra in its imperial court after a conquest in <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>. Influences from Middle East, Persia, India, Mongolia, and other countries followed. In fact, Chinese tradition attributes many musical instruments from this period to those regions and countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs207_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs207-61">[61]</a></sup> Cymbals gained popularity, along with more advanced trumpets, clarinets, pianos, oboes, flutes, drums, and lutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs218_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs218-62">[62]</a></sup> Some of the first <a href="/wiki/Bow_(music)" title="Bow (music)">bowed</a> zithers appeared in China in the 9th or 10th century, influenced by Mongolian culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs216_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs216-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>India experienced similar development to China in the post-classical era; however, stringed instruments developed differently as they accommodated different styles of music. While stringed instruments of China were designed to produce precise tones capable of matching the tones of chimes, stringed instruments of India were considerably more flexible. This flexibility suited the slides and <a href="/wiki/Tremolo" title="Tremolo">tremolos</a> of Hindu music. Rhythm was of paramount importance in Indian music of the time, as evidenced by the frequent depiction of drums in reliefs dating to the post-classical era. The emphasis on rhythm is an aspect native to Indian music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs221_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs221-64">[64]</a></sup> Historians divide the development of musical instruments in medieval India between pre-Islamic and Islamic periods due to the different influence each period provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs222_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs222-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>In pre-Islamic times, idiophones such as <a href="/wiki/Handbell" title="Handbell">handbells</a>, cymbals, and peculiar instruments resembling gongs came into wide use in Hindu music. The gong-like instrument was a bronze disk that was struck with a hammer instead of a mallet. Tubular drums, stick zithers (<a href="/wiki/Veena" title="Veena">veena</a>), short fiddles, double and triple flutes, coiled trumpets, and curved India horns emerged in this time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs223_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs223-66">[66]</a></sup> Islamic influences brought new types of drum, perfectly circular or octagonal as opposed to the irregular pre-Islamic drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs229_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs229-67">[67]</a></sup> Persian influence brought oboes and <a href="/wiki/Sitar" title="Sitar">sitars</a>, although Persian sitars had three strings and Indian version had from four to seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs231_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs231-68">[68]</a></sup> The Islamic culture also introduced double-<a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a> instruments as the <a href="/wiki/Alboka" title="Alboka">Alboka</a> (from Arab, al-buq or "horn") nowadays only alive in <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(autonomous_community)" title="Basque Country (autonomous community)">Basque Country</a>. It must be played using the technique of the circular breathing.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alboka.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="2972" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alboka.png/220px-Alboka.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alboka.png/330px-Alboka.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alboka.png/440px-Alboka.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alboka.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Alboka has a double-reed that vibrates when blown on the small tube. The tubes regulates the melody and the big horn amplifies the sound.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg/220px-Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg/330px-Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg/440px-Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_indonesian_instruments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Indonesian metallophone</div></div></div>
<p>Southeast Asian musical innovations include those during a period of Indian influence that ended around 920 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs236_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs236-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Balinese_people" title="Balinese people">Balinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a> music made use of <a href="/wiki/Xylophone" title="Xylophone">xylophones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metallophone" title="Metallophone">metallophones</a>, bronze versions of the former.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs238_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs238-70">[70]</a></sup> The most prominent and important musical instrument of Southeast Asia was the gong. While the gong likely originated in the geographical area between <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, it was part of every category of human activity in <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">maritime Southeast Asia</a> including <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs240_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs240-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The areas of Mesopotamia and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> experiences rapid growth and sharing of musical instruments once they were united by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a> in the seventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs246_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs246-72">[72]</a></sup> Frame drums and cylindrical drums of various depths were immensely important in all genres of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs249_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs249-73">[73]</a></sup> Conical oboes were involved in the music that accompanied wedding and circumcision ceremonies. Persian miniatures provide information on the development of <a href="/wiki/Timpani" title="Timpani">kettle drums</a> in Mesopotamia that spread as far as Java.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs250_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs250-74">[74]</a></sup> Various lutes, zithers, <a href="/wiki/Hammered_dulcimer" title="Hammered dulcimer">dulcimers</a>, and harps spread as far as <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> to the south and modern-day <a href="/wiki/Sulawesi" title="Sulawesi">Sulawesi</a> to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs251_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs251-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the influences of Greece and Rome, most musical instruments in Europe during the Middles Ages came from Asia. The lyre is the only musical instrument that may have been invented in Europe until this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs260_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs260-76">[76]</a></sup> Stringed instruments were prominent in Middle Age Europe. The central and northern regions used mainly lutes, stringed instruments with <a href="/wiki/Neck_(music)" title="Neck (music)">necks</a>, while the southern region used lyres, which featured a two-armed body and a crossbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs260_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs260-76">[76]</a></sup> Various harps served Central and Northern Europe as far north as Ireland, where the harp eventually became a national symbol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs263_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs263-77">[77]</a></sup> Lyres propagated through the same areas, as far east as <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs265_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs265-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>European music between 800 and 1100 became more sophisticated, more frequently requiring instruments capable of <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a>. The 9th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> geographer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khordadbeh" title="Ibn Khordadbeh">Ibn Khordadbeh</a> mentioned in his lexicographical discussion of music instruments that, in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, typical instruments included the <i>urghun</i> (<a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a>), <i>shilyani</i> (probably a type of <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>), <i>salandj</i> (probably a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagpipe" title="Bagpipe">bagpipe</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_lyra" title="Byzantine lyra">lyra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kartomi124_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kartomi124-79">[79]</a></sup> The Byzantine lyra, a <a href="/wiki/Bow_(music)" title="Bow (music)">bowed</a> string instrument, is an ancestor of most European bowed instruments, including the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grillet29_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grillet29-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Monochord" title="Monochord">monochord</a> served as a precise measure of the notes of a musical scale, allowing more accurate musical arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs269_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs269-81">[81]</a></sup> Mechanical <a href="/wiki/Hurdy-gurdy" title="Hurdy-gurdy">hurdy-gurdies</a> allowed single musicians to play more complicated arrangements than a fiddle would; both were prominent folk instruments in the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs271_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs271-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs274_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs274-83">[83]</a></sup> Southern Europeans played short and long lutes whose pegs extended to the sides, unlike the rear-facing pegs of Central and Northern European instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs273_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs273-84">[84]</a></sup> Idiophones such as bells and clappers served various practical purposes, such as warning of the approach of a <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs278_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs278-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ninth century revealed the first <a href="/wiki/Bagpipes" title="Bagpipes">bagpipes</a>, which spread throughout Europe and had many uses from folk instruments to military instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs281_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs281-86">[86]</a></sup> The construction of pneumatic <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organs</a> evolved in Europe starting in fifth-century <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, spreading to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in about 700.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs284_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs284-87">[87]</a></sup> The resulting instruments varied in size and use from portable organs worn around the neck to large pipe organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs286_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs286-88">[88]</a></sup> Literary accounts of organs being played in English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictine" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> abbeys toward the end of the tenth century are the first references to organs being connected to churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bicknell13_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bicknell13-89">[89]</a></sup> Reed players of the Middle Ages were limited to <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>; no evidence of <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinets</a> exists during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs288_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs288-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Classical">Western Classical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Western Classical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Musical instrument development was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Occident</a> from 1400 on, indeed, the most profound changes occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs297_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs297-18">[18]</a></sup> Instruments took on other purposes than accompanying singing or dance, and performers used them as solo instruments. Keyboards and lutes developed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphonic" title="Polyphonic">polyphonic</a> instruments, and composers arranged increasingly complex pieces using more advanced <a href="/wiki/Tablature" title="Tablature">tablature</a>. Composers also began designing pieces of music for specific instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs297_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs297-18">[18]</a></sup> In the latter half of the sixteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Orchestration" title="Orchestration">orchestration</a> came into common practice as a method of writing music for a variety of instruments. Composers now specified orchestration where individual performers once applied their own discretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs298_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs298-91">[91]</a></sup> The polyphonic style dominated popular music, and the instrument makers responded accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs351_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs351-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg/200px-The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg/300px-The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg/400px-The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Duet_c1635_by_Saftleven.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Duet</i>, by Dutch painter <a href="/wiki/Cornelis_Saftleven" title="Cornelis Saftleven">Cornelis Saftleven</a>, c. 1635. It shows a <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violinist</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cittern" title="Cittern">cittern</a> player.</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in about 1400, the rate of development of musical instruments increased in earnest as compositions demanded more dynamic sounds. People also began writing books about creating, playing, and cataloging musical instruments; the first such book was <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Virdung" title="Sebastian Virdung">Sebastian Virdung</a>'s 1511 treatise <i>Musica getuscht und ausgezogen</i> ('Music Germanized and Abstracted').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs298_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs298-91">[91]</a></sup> Virdung's work is noted as being particularly thorough for including descriptions of "irregular" instruments such as hunters' horns and cow bells, though Virdung is critical of the same. Other books followed, including <a href="/wiki/Arnolt_Schlick" title="Arnolt Schlick">Arnolt Schlick</a>'s <i>Spiegel der Orgelmacher und Organisten</i> ('Mirror of Organ Makers and Organ Players') the following year, a treatise on organ building and organ playing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs299_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs299-93">[93]</a></sup> Of the instructional books and references published in the Renaissance era, one is noted for its detailed description and depiction of all wind and stringed instruments, including their relative sizes. This book, the <i>Syntagma musicum</i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Praetorius" title="Michael Praetorius">Michael Praetorius</a>, is now considered an authoritative reference of sixteenth-century musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs301_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs301-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the sixteenth century, musical instrument builders gave most instruments – such as the violin – the "classical shapes" they retain today. An emphasis on aesthetic beauty also developed; listeners were as pleased with the physical appearance of an instrument as they were with its sound. Therefore, builders paid special attention to materials and workmanship, and instruments became collectibles in homes and museums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs302_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs302-95">[95]</a></sup> It was during this period that makers began constructing instruments of the same type in various sizes to meet the demand of <i>consorts</i>, or ensembles playing works written for these groups of instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs303_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs303-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Instrument builders developed other features that endure today. For example, while organs with multiple keyboards and pedals already existed, the first organs with <a href="/wiki/Organ_stop" title="Organ stop">solo stops</a> emerged in the early fifteenth century. These stops were meant to produce a mixture of timbres, a development needed for the complexity of music of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs307_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs307-97">[97]</a></sup> Trumpets evolved into their modern form to improve portability, and players used <a href="/wiki/Mute_(music)" title="Mute (music)">mutes</a> to properly blend into <a href="/wiki/Chamber_music" title="Chamber music">chamber music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs328_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs328-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Baroque">Baroque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Baroque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stainer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="719" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Stainer.jpg/220px-Stainer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Stainer.jpg/330px-Stainer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Stainer.jpg/440px-Stainer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stainer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> mounted <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Stainer" title="Jacob Stainer">Jacob Stainer</a> violin from 1658</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the seventeenth century, composers began writing works to a higher emotional degree. They felt that <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphony</a> better suited the emotional style they were aiming for and began writing musical parts for instruments that would complement the singing human voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs351_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs351-92">[92]</a></sup> As a result, many instruments that were incapable of larger ranges and dynamics, and therefore were seen as unemotional, fell out of favor. One such instrument was the shawm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs352_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs352-99">[99]</a></sup> Bowed instruments such as the violin, <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baryton" title="Baryton">baryton</a>, and various lutes dominated popular music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs353_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs353-100">[100]</a></sup> Beginning in around 1750, however, the lute disappeared from musical compositions in favor of the rising popularity of the <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs374_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs374-101">[101]</a></sup> As the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/String_orchestra" title="String orchestra">string orchestras</a> rose, wind instruments such as the flute, oboe, and bassoon were readmitted to counteract the monotony of hearing only strings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs380_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs380-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-seventeenth century, what was known as a hunter's horn underwent a transformation into an "art instrument" consisting of a lengthened tube, a narrower bore, a wider bell, and a much wider range. The details of this transformation are unclear, but the modern <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horn</a> or, more colloquially, French horn, had emerged by 1725.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs384_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs384-103">[103]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Slide_trumpet" title="Slide trumpet">slide trumpet</a> appeared, a variation that includes a long-throated mouthpiece that slid in and out, allowing the player infinite adjustments in <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a>. This variation on the trumpet was unpopular due to the difficulty involved in playing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs385_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs385-104">[104]</a></sup> Organs underwent tonal changes in the Baroque period, as manufacturers such as Abraham Jordan of London made the stops more expressive and added devices such as expressive pedals. Sachs viewed this trend as a "degeneration" of the general organ sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs386_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs386-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_and_Romantic">Classical and Romantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Classical and Romantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mozart_family_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3376" data-file-width="2864" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mozart_family_crop.jpg/200px-Mozart_family_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mozart_family_crop.jpg/300px-Mozart_family_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mozart_family_crop.jpg/400px-Mozart_family_crop.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mozart_family_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart</a> plays the keyboard while his father, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Mozart" title="Leopold Mozart">Leopold Mozart</a>, plays violin.</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">Romantic</a> periods of music, lasting from roughly 1750 to 1900, many musical instruments capable of producing new timbres and higher volume were developed and introduced into popular music. The design changes that broadened the quality of timbres allowed instruments to produce a wider variety of expression. Large orchestras rose in popularity and, in parallel, the composers determined to produce entire orchestral scores that made use of the expressive abilities of modern instruments. Since instruments were involved in collaborations of a much larger scale, their designs had to evolve to accommodate the demands of the orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs388_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs388-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some instruments also had to become louder to fill larger halls and be heard over sizable orchestras. Flutes and bowed instruments underwent many modifications and design changes—most of them unsuccessful—in efforts to increase volume. Other instruments were changed just so they could play their parts in the scores. Trumpets traditionally had a "defective" range—they were incapable of producing certain notes with precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs389_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs389-107">[107]</a></sup> New instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxophone" title="Saxophone">saxophone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tuba" title="Tuba">tuba</a> became fixtures in orchestras. Instruments such as the clarinet also grew into entire "families" of instruments capable of different ranges: small clarinets, normal clarinets, bass clarinets, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs388_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs388-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:199px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_boy_playing_the_violin_(I0002501).tiff"><img alt="A “young boy playing the violin.” Beside him is a table with likely a banjo on it." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3890" data-file-width="3042" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff/lossy-page1-197px-Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff/lossy-page1-295px-Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff/lossy-page1-394px-Young_boy_playing_the_violin_%28I0002501%29.tiff.jpg 2x" width="197"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_boy_playing_the_violin_(I0002501).tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A “young boy playing the violin” from Glengarry County, Ontario taken [between 1895 and 1910] from the Bartle Brothers fonds at the Archives of Ontario.</div></div></div>
<p>Accompanying the changes to timbre and volume was a shift in the typical pitch used to tune instruments. Instruments meant to play together, as in an orchestra, must be tuned to the same standard lest they produce audibly different sounds while playing the same notes. Beginning in 1762, the average <a href="/wiki/Concert_pitch" title="Concert pitch">concert pitch</a> began rising from a low of 377 vibrations to a high of 457 in 1880 Vienna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs390_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs390-108">[108]</a></sup> Different regions, countries, and even instrument manufacturers preferred different standards, making orchestral collaboration a challenge. Despite even the efforts of two organized international summits attended by noted composers like <a href="/wiki/Hector_Berlioz" title="Hector Berlioz">Hector Berlioz</a>, no standard could be agreed upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs391_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs391-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Twentieth_century_to_present">Twentieth century to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Twentieth century to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg/192px-FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg/288px-FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg/384px-FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg 2x" width="192"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FGF_museum_01._Leo_and_early_models.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fender_Musical_Instruments_Corporation" title="Fender Musical Instruments Corporation">Fender</a> brand <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitars</a></div></div></div>
<p>The evolution of traditional musical instruments slowed beginning in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remnant183_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remnant183-110">[110]</a></sup> Instruments such as the violin, flute, french horn, and harp are largely the same as those manufactured throughout the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries.
