Gradual iterations do emerge; for example, the "New Violin Family" began in 1964 to provide differently sized violins to expand the range of available sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remnant70_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remnant70-111">[111]</a></sup> The slowdown in development was a practical response to the concurrent slowdown in orchestra and venue size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs445_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs445-112">[112]</a></sup> Despite this trend in traditional instruments, the development of new musical instruments exploded in the twentieth century, and the variety of instruments developed overshadows any prior period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remnant183_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Remnant183-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> in the 20th century led to a new category of musical instruments: electronic instruments, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrophone" title="Electrophone">electrophones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs447_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs447-113">[113]</a></sup> The vast majority produced in the first half of the 20th century were what Sachs called "electromechanical instruments"; they have mechanical parts that produce sound vibrations picked up and amplified by electrical components. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Hammond_organ" title="Hammond organ">Hammond organs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs447_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs447-113">[113]</a></sup> Sachs also defined a subcategory of "radioelectric instruments" such as the <a href="/wiki/Theremin" title="Theremin">theremin</a>, which produces music through the player's hand movements around two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antennas" title="Antennas">antennas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs448_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs448-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg/200px-Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg/300px-Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg/400px-Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moog_Modular_55_img2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1975 <a href="/wiki/Moog_synthesizer" title="Moog synthesizer">Moog</a> Modular 55 synthesizer</div></div></div>
<p>The latter half of the 20th century saw the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a>, which produce sound using circuits and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microchips" title="Microchips">microchips</a>. In the late 1960s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Moog" title="Bob Moog">Bob Moog</a> and other inventors developed the first commercial synthesizers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Moog_synthesizer" title="Moog synthesizer">Moog synthesizer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinch7_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinch7-115">[115]</a></sup> Whereas once they had filled rooms, synthesizers can now be embedded in any electronic device,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinch7_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pinch7-115">[115]</a></sup> and are ubiquitous in modern music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-116">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sampler_(musical_instrument)" title="Sampler (musical instrument)">Samplers</a>, introduced around 1980, allow users to <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(music)" title="Sampling (music)">sample</a> and reuse existing sounds, and were important to the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> 1982 saw the introduction of <a href="/wiki/MIDI" title="MIDI">MIDI</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Standardization" title="Standardization">standardized</a> means of synchronizing electronic instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The modern proliferation of computers and microchips has created an industry of electronic musical instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning168_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manning168-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument_classification" title="Musical instrument classification">Musical instrument classification</a></div>
<p>There are many different methods of classifying musical instruments. Various methods examine aspects such as the physical properties of the instrument (material, color, shape, etc.), the use for the instrument, the means by which music is produced with the instrument, the <a href="/wiki/Range_(music)" title="Range (music)">range</a> of the instrument, and the instrument's place in an <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestra</a> or other ensemble. Most methods are specific to a geographic area or cultural group and were developed to serve the unique classification requirements of the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu210_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu210-120">[120]</a></sup> The problem with these specialized classification schemes is that they tend to break down once they are applied outside of their original area. For example, a system based on instrument use would fail if a culture invented a new use for the same instrument. Scholars recognize <a href="/wiki/Hornbostel%E2%80%93Sachs" title="Hornbostel–Sachs">Hornbostel–Sachs</a> as the only system that applies to any culture and, more importantly, provides the only possible classification for each instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu211_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu211-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kartomi176_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kartomi176-122">[122]</a></sup> The most common classifications are <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">strings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brass_instrument" title="Brass instrument">brass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodwind_instrument" title="Woodwind instrument">woodwind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Percussion_instrument" title="Percussion instrument">percussion</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_systems">Ancient systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ancient systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An ancient Hindu system named the <i><a href="/wiki/Natya_Shastra" title="Natya Shastra">Natya Shastra</a></i>, written by the sage <a href="/wiki/Bharata_Muni" title="Bharata Muni">Bharata Muni</a> and dating from between 200 BC and 200 AD, divides instruments into four main classification groups: instruments where the sound is produced by vibrating strings; percussion instruments with skin heads; instruments where the sound is produced by vibrating columns of air; and "solid", or non-skin, percussion instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu211_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu211-121">[121]</a></sup> This system was similar to some degree in 12th-century Europe by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_de_Muris" title="Johannes de Muris">Johannes de Muris</a>, who used the terms <i>tensibilia</i> (stringed instruments), <i>inflatibilia</i> (wind instruments), and <i>percussibilia</i> (all percussion instruments).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault190_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault190-123">[123]</a></sup> In 1880, <a href="/wiki/Victor-Charles_Mahillon" title="Victor-Charles Mahillon">Victor-Charles Mahillon</a> adapted the <i>Natya Shastra</i> and assigned Greek labels to the four classifications: <i>chordophones</i> (stringed instruments), <i>membranophones</i> (skin-head percussion instruments), <i>aerophones</i> (wind instruments), and <i>autophones</i> (non-skin percussion instruments).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu211_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu211-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hornbostel.E2.80.93Sachs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hornbostel–Sachs">Hornbostel–Sachs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hornbostel–Sachs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Erich_von_Hornbostel" title="Erich von Hornbostel">Erich von Hornbostel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curt_Sachs" title="Curt Sachs">Curt Sachs</a> adopted Mahillon's scheme and published an extensive new scheme for classification in <i>Zeitschrift für Ethnologie</i> in 1914. Hornbostel and Sachs used most of Mahillon's system, but replaced the term <i>autophone</i> with <i>idiophone</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montagu211_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-Montagu211-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original <a href="/wiki/Hornbostel%E2%80%93Sachs" title="Hornbostel–Sachs">Hornbostel–Sachs</a> system classified instruments into four main groups:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Idiophone" title="Idiophone">Idiophones</a>, which produce sound by vibrating the primary body of the instrument itself; they are sorted into concussion, percussion, shaken, scraped, split, and plucked idiophones, such as <a href="/wiki/Claves" title="Claves">claves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xylophone" title="Xylophone">xylophone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guiro" title="Guiro">guiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slit_drum" title="Slit drum">slit drum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mbira" title="Mbira">mbira</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rattle_(percussion_instrument)" title="Rattle (percussion instrument)">rattle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse3_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse3-124">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Membranophone" title="Membranophone">Membranophones</a>, which produce sound by a vibrating a stretched membrane; they may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drums" title="Drums">drums</a> (further sorted by the shape of the shell), which are struck by hand, with a stick, or rubbed, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kazoos" title="Kazoos">kazoos</a> and other instruments that use a stretched membrane for the primary sound (not simply to modify sound produced in another way) are also considered membranophones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse117_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse117-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordophone" title="Chordophone">Chordophones</a>, which produce sound by vibrating one or more strings; they are sorted according to the relationship between the string(s) and the sounding board or chamber. For example, if the strings are laid out parallel to the sounding board and there is no neck, the instrument is a <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zither</a> whether it is plucked like an <a href="/wiki/Autoharp" title="Autoharp">autoharp</a> or struck with hammers like a <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>. If the instrument has strings parallel to the sounding board or chamber and the strings extend past the board with a neck, then the instrument is a <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lute</a>, whether the sound chamber is constructed of wood like a <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a> or uses a membrane like a <a href="/wiki/Banjo" title="Banjo">banjo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse177_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse177-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aerophone" title="Aerophone">Aerophones</a>, which produce a sound with a vibrating column of air; they are sorted into free aerophones such as a <a href="/wiki/Bullroarer" title="Bullroarer">bullroarer</a> or <a href="/wiki/Whip" title="Whip">whip</a>, which move freely through the air; reedless aerophones such as <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flutes</a> and recorders, which cause the air to pass over a sharp edge; reed instruments, which use a vibrating reed (this category may be further divided into two classifications: single-reeded and double-reeded instruments. Examples of the former are clarinets and saxophones, while the latter includes oboes and bassoons); and lip-vibrated aerophones such as <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a>, trombones and tubas, for which the lips themselves function as vibrating reeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcuse549_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcuse549-127">[127]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Sachs later added a fifth category, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrophone" title="Electrophone">electrophones</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Theremin" title="Theremin">theremins</a>, which produce sound by electronic means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs447_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs447-113">[113]</a></sup> Within each category are many subgroups. The system has been criticised and revised over the years, but remains widely used by <a href="/wiki/Ethnomusicology" title="Ethnomusicology">ethnomusicologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organology" title="Organology">organologists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault190_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rault190-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell39_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell39-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schaeffner">Schaeffner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Schaeffner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Andre Schaeffner, a curator at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Homme" title="Musée de l'Homme">Musée de l'Homme</a>, disagreed with the Hornbostel–Sachs system and developed his own system in 1932. Schaeffner believed that the pure physics of a musical instrument, rather than its specific construction or playing method, should always determine its classification. (Hornbostel–Sachs, for example, divides aerophones on the basis of sound production, but membranophones on the basis of the shape of the instrument). His system divided instruments into two categories: instruments with solid, vibrating bodies and instruments containing vibrating air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kartomi174_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kartomi174-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range">Range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Range_(music)" title="Range (music)">Range (music)</a></div>
<p>Musical instruments are also often classified by their musical range in comparison with other instruments in the same family. This exercise is useful when placing instruments in context of an orchestra or other ensemble.
</p><p>These terms are named after singing voice classifications:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">Soprano</a> instruments: <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soprano_saxophone" title="Soprano saxophone">soprano saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piccolo" title="Piccolo">piccolo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alto" title="Alto">Alto</a> instruments: <a href="/wiki/Alto_saxophone" title="Alto saxophone">alto saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">french horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alto_flute" title="Alto flute">alto flute</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_horn" title="English horn">english horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alto_clarinet" title="Alto clarinet">alto clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alto_horn" title="Alto horn">alto horn</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">Tenor</a> instruments: <a href="/wiki/Trombone" title="Trombone">trombone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenoroon" title="Tenoroon">tenoroon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenor_saxophone" title="Tenor saxophone">tenor saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenor_violin" title="Tenor violin">tenor violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenor_drum" title="Tenor drum">tenor drum</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baritone" title="Baritone">Baritone</a> instruments: <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baritone_saxophone" title="Baritone saxophone">baritone saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baritone_horn" title="Baritone horn">baritone horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euphonium" title="Euphonium">euphonium</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bass_(sound)" title="Bass (sound)">Bass</a> instruments: <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">double bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_guitar" title="Bass guitar">bass guitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contrabassoon" title="Contrabassoon">contrabassoon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_saxophone" title="Bass saxophone">bass saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuba" title="Tuba">tuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_drum" title="Bass drum">bass drum</a></li></ul>
<p>Some instruments fall into more than one category. For example, the cello may be considered tenor, baritone or bass, depending on how its music fits into the ensemble. The trombone and French horn may be alto, tenor, baritone, or bass depending on the range it is played in. Many instruments have their range as part of their name: <a href="/wiki/Soprano_saxophone" title="Soprano saxophone">soprano saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenor_saxophone" title="Tenor saxophone">tenor saxophone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baritone_horn" title="Baritone horn">baritone horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alto_flute" title="Alto flute">alto flute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_guitar" title="Bass guitar">bass guitar</a>, etc. Additional adjectives describe instruments above the soprano range or <a href="/wiki/Contrabass" title="Contrabass">below the bass</a>, for example the <a href="/wiki/Sopranino_saxophone" title="Sopranino saxophone">sopranino saxophone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contrabass_clarinet" title="Contrabass clarinet">contrabass clarinet</a>. When used in the name of an instrument, these terms are relative, describing the instrument's range in comparison to other instruments of its family and not in comparison to the human voice range or instruments of other families. For example, a bass flute's range is from C<sub>3</sub> to F♯<sub>6</sub>, while a bass clarinet plays about one octave lower.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2232" data-file-width="3968" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg/220px-African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg/330px-African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg/440px-African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_music_instrument_made_from_a_can_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>African <a href="/wiki/Mbira" title="Mbira">kalimba</a> made from a food can</div></div></div>
<p>The materials used in making musical instruments vary greatly by culture and application. Many of the materials have special significance owing to their source or rarity. Some cultures worked substances from the human body into their instruments. In ancient Mexico, for example, the material drums were made from might contain actual human body parts obtained from sacrificial offerings. In New Guinea, drum makers would mix human blood into the adhesive used to attach the <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_membrane" title="Acoustic membrane">membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault184_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault184-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Morus_(plant)" title="Morus (plant)">Mulberry</a> trees are held in high regard in China owing to their mythological significance—instrument makers would hence use them to make zithers. The <a href="/wiki/Yakuts" title="Yakuts">Yakuts</a> believe that making drums from trees struck by lightning gives them a special connection to nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault185_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault185-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Five_string_kanteles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Five_string_kanteles.jpg/220px-Five_string_kanteles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Five_string_kanteles.jpg/330px-Five_string_kanteles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Five_string_kanteles.jpg/440px-Five_string_kanteles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Five_string_kanteles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two five string <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finnish</a> <a href="/wiki/Kantele" title="Kantele">kanteles</a>. Shape of the upper kantele is more traditional, while the one for kantele below is slightly modernised</div></div></div>
<p>Musical instrument construction is a specialized trade that requires years of training, practice, and sometimes an apprenticeship. Most makers of musical instruments specialize in one genre of instruments; for example, a <a href="/wiki/Luthier" title="Luthier">luthier</a> makes only stringed instruments. Some make only one type of instrument such as a piano. Whatever the instrument constructed, the instrument maker must consider materials, construction technique, and decoration, creating a balanced instrument that is both functional and aesthetically pleasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rault195_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rault195-132">[132]</a></sup> Some builders are focused on a more artistic approach and develop <a href="/wiki/Experimental_musical_instrument" title="Experimental musical instrument">experimental musical instruments</a>, often meant for individual playing styles developed by the builder themself.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="User_interfaces">User interfaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: User interfaces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Usnaconsole2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usnaconsole2.jpg/220px-Usnaconsole2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usnaconsole2.jpg/330px-Usnaconsole2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Usnaconsole2.jpg/440px-Usnaconsole2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Usnaconsole2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The five-manual, 522-stop detached console at the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Academy_Chapel" title="Naval Academy Chapel">United States Naval Academy Chapel</a> crafted by R. A. Colby, Inc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Regardless of how the sound is produced, many musical instruments have a keyboard as the user interface. <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_instrument" title="Keyboard instrument">Keyboard instruments</a> are any instruments that are played with a <a href="/wiki/Musical_keyboard" title="Musical keyboard">musical keyboard</a>, which is a row of small keys that can be pressed. Every key generates one or more sounds; most keyboard instruments have extra means (<a href="/wiki/Piano#Pedals" title="Piano">pedals</a> for a piano, <a href="/wiki/Organ_stop" title="Organ stop">stops</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pedal_keyboard" title="Pedal keyboard">pedal keyboard</a> for an organ) to manipulate these sounds. They may produce sound by wind being fanned (<a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a>) or pumped (<a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bicknell_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-bicknell-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard-135">[135]</a></sup> vibrating strings either hammered (<a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>) or plucked (<a href="/wiki/Harpsichord" title="Harpsichord">harpsichord</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fine_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-fine-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> by electronic means (<a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizer</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paradiso_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-paradiso-138">[138]</a></sup> or in some other way. Sometimes, instruments that do not usually have a keyboard, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Glockenspiel" title="Glockenspiel">glockenspiel</a></i>, are fitted with one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vslglocken_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-vslglocken-139">[139]</a></sup> Though they have no moving parts and are struck by mallets held in the player's hands, they have the same physical arrangement of keys and produce soundwaves in a similar manner. The <a href="/wiki/Theremin" title="Theremin">theremin</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrophone" title="Electrophone">electrophone</a>, is played without physical contact by the player. The theremin senses the proximity of the player's hands, which triggers changes in its sound. More recently, a <a href="/wiki/MIDI_controller" title="MIDI controller">MIDI controller</a> keyboard used with a <a href="/wiki/Digital_audio_workstation" title="Digital audio workstation">digital audio workstation</a> may have a musical keyboard and a bank of sliders, knobs, and buttons that change many sound parameters of a <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizer</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Instrumentalist">Instrumentalist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Musical_instrument&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Instrumentalist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Instrumentalist" redirects here. For the philosophical position on science, see <a href="/wiki/Instrumentalism" title="Instrumentalism">Instrumentalism</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_musicians#Instrument" title="Lists of musicians">Lists of musicians § Instrument</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Multi-instrumentalist" title="Multi-instrumentalist">Multi-instrumentalist</a></div>
<p>A person who plays a musical instrument is known as an instrumentalist or <a href="/wiki/Musician#Types" title="Musician">instrumental musician</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Many instrumentalists are known for playing specific musical instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Guitarist" title="Guitarist">guitarist</a> (<a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pianist" title="Pianist">pianist</a> (<a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bassist" title="Bassist">bassist</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bass_(instrument)" title="Bass (instrument)">bass</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Drummer" title="Drummer">drummer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drum</a>). These different types of instrumentalists can perform together in a music group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> A person who is able to play a number of instruments is called a multi-instrumentalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> According to David Baskerville in the book <i>Music Business Handbook and Career Guide</i>, the working hours of a full-time instrumentalist may average only three hours a day, but most musicians spent at least forty hours a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
